    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="4b434b45413e4333b5f4df8d1a72c199">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '7862be6c-a940-4679-95cb-ac12b392b4f5'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d45008455" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Single-atom metals (SAMs), despite being promising for high-utilization catalysis, biomedicine, and energy storage, usually suffer from limited catalytic performance caused by low metal loading. Herein, via an oriented diffusion strategy, all-region ultrahigh-loading (18.9&nbsp;wt.%) Sn-SAMs over carbon nanorings matrix (Sn-SAMs@CNR) are initially achieved based on the transformation of a g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@SnO<sub>2</sub>@polydopamine ring-like nested structure. The formation process of Sn-SAMs involves a critical conversion from oxygen-coordination (SnO<sub>2</sub>) to nitrogen-coordination (Sn-N<sub>4</sub>) and simultaneous anti-Osterwalder ripening promoted under spatial confinement. Notably, the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>-derived N-containing gaseous intermediates dynamically drive the oriented diffusion (inside-out diffusion) of Sn-SAMs across the carbon nanorings, realizing an all-region ultrahigh loading of SAMs throughout the carbon matrix. This strategy is also applied to other metal materials (Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, and Sb), and features excellent universality. When applied as the anode for sodium-ion batteries, experimental analyses and theoretical calculations demonstrate that high-loading Sn-N<sub>4</sub> active sites significantly optimize electron density distribution and improve reaction kinetics. Consequently, Sn-SAMs@CNR exhibits outstanding durability of 364&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> &nbsp; even after 5000 cycles with an impressively low (0.00068%) capacity decay per cycle. This work opens up a universally new avenue for all-region ultrahigh loading of SAMs to carbon matrix for high-performance energy storage.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202412592-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202412592-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Single-atom metals (SAMs) with individual metal atoms securely anchored to supports can maximize the utilization of the metal for catalysis and energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0001_R_d45008328e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Catal.">1</a>, <a href="#adma202412592-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0002_R_d45008328e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Commun.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with nanoparticles and bulk metals, the robust metal-matrix interaction and quantum size effect endow SAMs with unique chemical, physical, and electronic characteristics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0003_R_d45008328e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">3</a>, <a href="#adma202412592-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0004_R_d45008328e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Since the groundbreaking discovery of SAMs by Zhang et&nbsp;al.,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0005_R_d45008328e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Chem.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> numerous experimental and theoretical investigations have witnessed the superiority of SAMs in the fields of electrocatalysis, thermocatalysis, photocatalysis, and biomedicine.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0006_R_d45008328e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Carbon as the most used support matrix can afford strong anchoring to metal atoms by forming robust chemical bonds and enable&nbsp;superior tunability, stability, and electrical conductivity. Despite the efficacy of several effective approaches in creating SAMs, including defect engineering, coordination design, spatial confinement, etc., they often invoke tedious multi-step processes and poor universality.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0007_R_d45008328e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, as the metal loading increases, SAMs tend to coalesce into sizable clusters or nanoparticles due to the higher free energy,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0008_R_d45008328e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> leading to low metal-atom density, typically below 3.0&nbsp;wt.%, greatly hindering their practical application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0009_R_d45008328e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, it is imperative to explore a straightforward and universal synthesis approach toward ultrahigh metal-atom-density (over 10.0&nbsp;wt.%) SAMs materials.</p>
         
         <p>SAMs are conventionally synthesized via bottom-up methods that employ synthetic metal alongside organic precursors, including metal–organic frameworks (MOFs), metal-porphyrin compounds, or small organic substances, with a subsequent carbonization treatment to create metal atom-embedded carbon skeletons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0010_R_d45008328e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite achieving higher metal loadings, the scalability of these methods is constrained by the reliance on costly organic ligands and the necessity of acid etching to eliminate nanoclusters or nanoparticles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0007_R_d45008328e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Conversely, there has been an increasing emphasis on top-down transformation, where the bulk metal, metal oxide, or metal sulfide is atomized directly onto carbon supports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0011_R_d45008328e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Environ. Sci.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, the top-down preparation of SAMs requires additional energy input to disrupt the metallic chemical bonds within the bulk ensemble to promote anti-Osterwalder ripening, so it is thermodynamically more challenging than the bottom-up approach.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0012_R_d45008328e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fortunately, utilizing the top-down fabrication route is more conducive to guaranteeing the steadiness of SAMs in harsh environments, opening up new possibilities for cost-effective manufacturing and scalable production.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0013_R_d45008328e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Typically, this route involves three sequence steps, the energy-driven detachment of surface metal atoms, the forced migration of debonded atoms, and atom-anchoring by surface defects on the matrix.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0014_R_d45008328e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Matter">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, Li et&nbsp;al. demonstrated that Pd metal nanoparticles could be converted to Pd-SAMs (loading of 0.16&nbsp;wt.%) utilizing MOFs-derived N-doped carbon as the matrix, forming a more thermodynamically stable Pd-N<sub>4</sub> structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0015_R_d45008328e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Yang et&nbsp;al. further illustrated that Ni nanoparticles located on the surface of N-doped carbon nanotube (CNT) can be converted into single surface-bound Ni atoms (loading of 0.68&nbsp;wt.%) by strong interaction between the surficial Ni atoms and N-rich defects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0016_R_d45008328e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nonetheless, certain thorny issues still need to be addressed for the top-down approach. The detachment of metal atoms from bulk materials usually involves high temperatures (≈900&nbsp;°C) and the use of corrosive gases (e.g., NH<sub>3</sub>, PH<sub>3</sub>, and HCl), posing great challenges for the device fabrication and property optimization of individual atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0017_R_d45008328e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the motion of debonded atoms before being trapped by the matrix is random and non-oriented under open space, which makes them dynamically anchored to surficial rather than interior defects of carbon scaffolds, inhibiting the internal loading.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0011_R_d45008328e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Environ. Sci.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, single-atom loading in this manner is usually restricted to less than 5.0&nbsp;wt.%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0018_R_d45008328e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Chem.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> So far, achieving the all-region loading of single atoms remains challenging, which needs to delicately engineer the top-down approach.</p>
         
         <p>Among the application realms of SAMs, compared with catalysis, energy storage is still relatively under-cultivated, especially sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Currently, carbon-based materials are the mainstream choice as anodes for SIBs due to the cost-effectiveness and abundance of raw materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0019_R_d45008328e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, they generally suffer from undesirable specific capacity and unsatisfactory long-term lifespan, caused by inherent lack of active sites and sluggish reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0020_R_d45008328e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although strategies including heteroatom (e.g., N, P, S, etc.) doping, defect engineering, and pore structure manipulation can enrich pseudocapacitive active sites in carbonaceous anode materials, they often reduce the electronic conductivity of carbon materials by introducing sp<sup>3</sup> hybridization and disrupting the π-conjugated system.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0021_R_d45008328e602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the “trap effect,” namely the high diffusion barrier and strong binding energy between defect sites and Na<sup>+</sup>, can cause a large proportion of irreversible reactions and a decrease in the initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0022_R_d45008328e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, SAMs-doping, particularly high-density SAMs in carbonaceous materials, can more effectively modulate the charge density and electronic structure between metal atoms and coordination atoms throughout the reaction processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0023_R_d45008328e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the relevant studies are still uncommon in SIBs, which deserve an in-depth exploration.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, based on the nanoengineering design (<b>Scheme</b> <a href="#adma202412592-fig-0006">1</a>) of a unique nested structure of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub>/polydopamine (PDA), we achieve an all-region ultrahigh Sn-SAMs loading of up to 18.9&nbsp;wt.%, distinctly surpassing the limitations of traditional surface/near-surface loading methods. The formation process of Sn-SAMs involves coordination-conversion (from oxygen to nitrogen coordination) and dynamically-driven oriented diffusion by g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanorings-derived gaseous intermediates across all the regions of the hollow carbon nanorings (CNRs) under spatial confinement. Notably, this innovative method provides a universal and scalable way to synthesize various single atoms (including Sn, Sb, Fe, Co, Ni, and Cu) with high loading over 10.0&nbsp;wt.%. Noticeably, the high-loading Sn-SAMs on CNRs (Sn-SAMs@CNR) used as the anode for SIBs show superior electrochemical performance, such as outstanding durability of 364&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even after 5000 cycles coupled with an impressive 0.00068% capacity decay per cycle, a high specific capacity (462&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and excellent rate capability (248&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). In/ex situ analysis reveals that incorporating high-density Sn-SAMs can substantially enhance the optimization of active sites and improve reaction kinetics significantly. Furthermore, density functional theory (DFT) calculations demonstrate that high-loading Sn-N<sub>4</sub> active sites can greatly enhance Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance via optimizing electron density distribution and improving adsorption capacity. This work establishes a general pathway for developing high-loading monodispersed atomic materials and proposes a comprehensive analysis of high-density Sn-SAMs-doping CNRs for high-performance SIBs.</p>

            <figure class="figure" id="adma202412592-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/edf985dd-c8f0-4e64-a169-53f0ae96ceeb/adma202412592-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/edf985dd-c8f0-4e64-a169-53f0ae96ceeb/adma202412592-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0fd8022f-4952-4168-b094-a4046b5ac437/adma202412592-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/edf985dd-c8f0-4e64-a169-53f0ae96ceeb/adma202412592-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
