      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202412592-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202412592-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202412592-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202412592-sec-0030-title">2.1 Materials Synthesis and Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The schematic diagram in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0001">1a</a> depicts the synthesis of Sn-SAMs@CNR. Initially, g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanorings were synthesized employing SiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres as substrates via chemical vapor deposition with a selective deposition effect (detail in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0024_R_d45008328e713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Storage Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the scanning electron microscope (SEM, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0001">1b</a>) exhibits a ring-like structure with an outer diameter of ≈200&nbsp;nm. Subsequently, SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles were homogeneously loaded onto the surface of the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanorings through a hydrothermal method, forming g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0001">1c</a>). Then a PDA layer was coated, leading to the development of a nanoring-like nested structure of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub>/PDA, with g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> as the filling, SnO<sub>2</sub> as the middle layer, and polymer as the outer layer. Following the 700&nbsp;°C high-temperature pyrolysis, Sn-SAMs@CNR was synthesized and maintained the nanoring architecture with hollow properties (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0001">1d</a>), due to the self-sacrifice of the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> template. The ring-like morphology of Sn-SAMs@CNR is more clearly visible through transmission electron microscopy (TEM, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0001">1e</a>) observation. The atomic resolution high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0001">1f</a>) image of Sn-SAMs@CNR reveals numerous bright spots at the atomic scale, indicative of isolated Sn atoms. Energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mapping images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0001">1g–k</a> demonstrate the even distribution of carbon, nitrogen, oxygen, and tin elements on Sn-SAMs@CNR.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202412592-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d8b85181-ffb2-4f95-828a-81ed9e4542e7/adma202412592-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d8b85181-ffb2-4f95-828a-81ed9e4542e7/adma202412592-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1ea325fb-da59-45a6-afd1-4aa8fe1e789b/adma202412592-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d8b85181-ffb2-4f95-828a-81ed9e4542e7/adma202412592-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202412592-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202412592" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic diagram of the preparation process of Sn-SAMs@CNR. SEM images of b) g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanorings, c) g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub>, and d) Sn-SAMs@CNR. e) TEM image and f) atomic resolution HAADF-STEM image of Sn-SAMs@CNR. g) HAADF-STEM image and EDS elemental mapping of h) carbon, i) nitrogen, j) oxygen, and k) tin for Sn-SAMs@CNR. l) Sn K-edge XANES spectra and m) the corresponding Fourier transformed (FT) <i>k</i><sup>3</sup>-weight EXAFS spectra of Sn-SAMs@CNR, SnO<sub>2</sub>, and Sn foil. n) EXAFS fitting curve of Sn-SAMs@CNR.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The chemical state of Sn-SAMs@CNR and the local coordination structure of Sn species were further studied at the atomic level using X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS). Analyses of the Sn K-edge X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0001">1l</a>) indicate that the line position of Sn-SAMs@CNR is situated between Sn foil and SnO<sub>2</sub>, suggesting a valence state of Sn ranging from 0 to +4.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0025_R_d45008328e811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fourier transform (FT) EXAFS analyses (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0001">1m</a>) show that the primary peak for R-space of Sn-SAMs@CNR at ≈1.50&nbsp;Å corresponds to the Sn-N(O) scattering path, indicating single atom dispersion in the sample.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0026_R_d45008328e820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fitting curves (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0001">1n</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) and corresponding EXAFS parameters (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) show an average coordination number of 3.9 for Sn-SAMs with a coordination distance of 2.12&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, the EXAFS wavelet transform (WT) plots (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) indicate a significant signal at 4.17&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup> for Sn-SAMs@CNR, smaller than those of Sn foil and SnO<sub>2</sub>, supporting the formation as Sn-N.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0027_R_d45008328e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Overall, the analyses confirm the formation of the Sn-N<sub>4</sub> structure through coordinated actions of the Sn-SAMs@CNR, as supported by EXAFS along with previous literature reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0013_R_d45008328e852" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202412592-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202412592-sec-0040-title">2.2 Formation Mechanism of Sn Single Atoms from SnO<sub>2</sub></h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the formation mechanism of exceptionally high loading of single atoms in Sn-SAMs@CNR, a comprehensive set of characterizations was conducted. X-ray diffraction (XRD, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) patterns reveal characteristic peaks of both SnO<sub>2</sub> and g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> in g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub>/PDA composite before high-temperature pyrolysis. After pyrolysis at 700&nbsp;°C for 40&nbsp;min, it is observed that characteristic peaks of both SnO<sub>2</sub> and g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> disappear, while a broad graphite peak appears. Raman spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) show that the intensity ratio of D band to G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub><i>/I</i><sub>G</sub>) increases from 0.857 to 1.005 during the pyrolysis process of the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub>/PDA composite, lasting from 10 to 40&nbsp;min. This increase confirms that the persistent defects stem from the impact and etching of abundant N-containing gas formed through g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> decomposition within the carbon layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0024_R_d45008328e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Storage Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0002">2a</a>,<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0002">b</a>) was applied to investigate the surface elemental composition of the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub>/PDA composite during thermal conversion. Remarkably, an increase in surface N content from 9.32 to 10.4&nbsp;at.% is observed as the pyrolysis time extended from 10 to 20&nbsp;min (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), signaling the initiation of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> disintegration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0028_R_d45008328e938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Appl. Catal. B Environ.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon reaching 30&nbsp;min, the surface N content sharply rises to 15.39&nbsp;at.%, indicating that g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> violently decomposes and diffuses outward driven by the intense kinetic energy of N-containing gas. Subsequently, at 40&nbsp;min, the surface N content decreases to 13.59&nbsp;at.%, suggesting the nearly complete breakup of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanorings to form a composite with a hollow structure, as supported by the SEM image of Sn-SAMs@CNR (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0001">1d</a>) and XRD patterns (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Similar trends are observed about the surface Sn metal content, with measured values increasing from 0.22 to 0.65&nbsp;at.% at pyrolysis times of 10 and 20&nbsp;min, respectively, attributed to the driving force by inside-out diffusion of N-containing gases, which coordinate SnO<sub>2</sub> to form intermediate Sn─O─N clusters and thrust them into the carbon layer. Upon reaching 30&nbsp;min of thermal cracking, the Sn─O─N moieties, driven by the kinetic energy from N-containing gas, undergo rapid oriented diffusion and atomization, resulting in a sharp increase in surface Sn content to 2.24&nbsp;at.%. Subsequently, at 40&nbsp;min, as g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> breakdown nears completion, a slight additional increase in surface Sn content to 2.39&nbsp;at.% is observed. XPS analyses reveal that the Sn content on the surface of the final product corresponds to a mass fraction of 18.5&nbsp;wt.%. Moreover, the atomic content of N in the analysis exceeds that of Sn by more than fourfold, favorably facilitating the formation of Sn─N<sub>4</sub> coordination.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0029_R_d45008328e967" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the Sn 3d spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0002">2c</a>) of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4/</sub>SnO<sub>2</sub>/PDA composite during the pyrolysis process, two sets of peaks at 495.3/486.9 and 494.8/486.3&nbsp;eV correspond to Sn─N and Sn<sup>4+</sup>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0030_R_d45008328e985" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Obviously, the intensity of the Sn─N bond intensifies as the pyrolysis advances, providing direct evidence that the strong Lewis acid-base interaction between N-containing gases and Sn is occurring and is the main driving force for the formation of Sn single atoms with stable Sn─N coordination sites.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202412592-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/283976bc-c2a8-4ac4-a017-a00ecfed3eed/adma202412592-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/283976bc-c2a8-4ac4-a017-a00ecfed3eed/adma202412592-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/36ca50ae-bb2e-4ecb-8b8b-d85aae6e619d/adma202412592-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/283976bc-c2a8-4ac4-a017-a00ecfed3eed/adma202412592-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202412592-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202412592" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Total XPS spectra, b) the surface N and Sn contents, and c) high-resolution Sn 3d spectra of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub>/PDA composite during the pyrolysis process at 700&nbsp;°C. d) DTG-DSC analyses and e) TG-MS spectra of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/PDA, g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub>/PDA, and g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub> composites. f) N 1s XPS spectra of CNR and Sn-SAMs@CNR. g) Metal loadings achieved by our oriented diffusion strategy on carbon supports in comparison with other top-down methods.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The pyrolysis process involving g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4/</sub>SnO<sub>2</sub>/PDA nested composite was further evaluated using derivative thermogravimetry-differential scanning calorimeter (DTG-DSC) analyses (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0002">2d</a>). Three contrast samples were prepared: g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/PDA (without SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles), g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub> (without PDA layer), and individual g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanorings. The DTG curve shows that the decomposition of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/PDA initiates ≈510&nbsp;°C, primarily attributed to the beginning of the decomposition of internal g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0024_R_d45008328e1072" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Storage Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A significant peak at 664&nbsp;°C in the DTG curve, accompanied by a distinct endothermic peak and weak peaks in the DSC curve, indicates the rupture of carbon-nitrogen bonds at high temperatures, leading to the breakdown of the layered structure of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>. The broader main peak in the DSC curve compared to individual g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanorings (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) suggests that the PDA coating layer's confinement effect renders the inner g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> filling less stable and more susceptible to degradation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0024_R_d45008328e1093" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Storage Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Conversely, analysis of the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub>/PDA composite reveals decomposition starting at ≈440&nbsp;°C, substantially lower than that of the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/PDA composite, mainly due to the coordination reaction between Sn-metal species and N-containing products promoting the breakup of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>. Moreover, the DTG curve displays three distinct peaks at ≈480, 530, and 590&nbsp;°C, together with endothermic peaks in the DSC, revealing a transition to a stagewise decomposition. The DTG-DSC analysis of another contrast sample of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub> composite without an outermost polymer coating also exhibits three characteristic peaks similar to those of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub>/PDA composite, but with peak positions shifts to higher temperatures and the main peak width of DSC narrows to the level of individual g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanorings. This suggests that the outermost nanoring-like polymer capsules can ensure effective and sufficient contact with spatially confined pyrolysis, creating favorable conditions for single atomization reactions. Thermogravimetric-mass spectroscopy (TG-MS, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0002">2e</a>) analyses show that the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/PDA material releases various gases, such as NH<sub>3</sub>, HCN, CO<sub>2</sub>, and H<sub>2</sub>O during the pyrolysis process, with peak release observed between 520 and 700&nbsp;°C, corresponding to the breakdown of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0031_R_d45008328e1149" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This increase in gas volume leads to the formation of abundant pores and defects in the carbon shell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0024_R_d45008328e1155" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Storage Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The introduction of SnO<sub>2</sub> in the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub>/PDA material leads to a shift in the decomposition behavior to lower temperatures, with the thermal breakdown process occurring in two steps. The first step, at 480&nbsp;°C, involves partial thermal degradation and loss of crystalline water, accompanied by the generation of small quantities of CO<sub>2</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>O, and NH<sub>3</sub>. The second step, ≈550&nbsp;°C, encompasses the complete decomposition of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>. A similar trend is observed for the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub> composite, where the lack of a PDA outer layer results in a higher decay temperature. The N in NH<sub>3</sub> and HCN exhibits notable coordination ability, facilitating the conversion from O-coordination to N-coordination and enabling the atomization of Sn. In addition, Sn-SAMs can also be synthesized by coating and pyrolyzing glucose (without N) instead of dopamine, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), revealing that the outermost polymer layer serves to create spatially confined pyrolysis conditions and functions as a carbon matrix precursor.</p>
            
            <p>Chemical bond properties were obtained through the XPS analyses of Sn-SAMs@CNR and CNR derived from the pyrolysis of the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/PDA composite. The N 1s spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0002">2f</a>) of CNR exhibits distinct peaks at 397.9, 399.3, 400.3, and 402.8&nbsp;eV with corresponding values of pyridine-N, pyrrole-N, graphite-N, and oxidized-N.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0032_R_d45008328e1201" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the N 1s spectrum of Sn-SAMs@CNR exhibits a shift in the binding energy of pyridine-N to 398.2&nbsp;eV, while the other peaks remain relatively stable. This shift is attributed to the ion withdrawing effect caused by metal ions replacing protons in pyridine-N, providing robust evidence for the coordination effect between pyridine-N and Sn.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0033_R_d45008328e1207" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The similarity of C 1s and O 1s spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information) of Sn-SAMs@CNR and CNR indicates that Sn does not significantly coordinate with C and O. Based on the above results, the oriented diffusion strategy is illustrated in detail in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). During high-temperature pyrolysis of the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub>/PDA nested structure, the Lewis basic N-species (NH<sub>3</sub>, HCN, etc.) with strong coordination ability produced by the decomposition of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> bind with Sn atoms in the middle layer, promoting the conversion from O-coordination (SnO<sub>2</sub>) to N-coordination (Sn-N<sub>4</sub>) and transferring their kinetic energy to the generated active Sn─O─N clusters. Subsequently, the energy-driven Sn─O─N moieties diffuse outward into the CNRs matrix and atomize, facilitating the all-region distribution of Sn-SAMs on the carbon substrate. Meanwhile, the outermost nanoring-like PDA capsules with spatially confined effects can ensure an effective and sufficient single-atomization process without the need for additional post-treatment acid washing. As a result, the nested structure transforms into a hollow CNRs structure with an impressive metal loading of 18.9&nbsp;wt.% for Sn-SAMs conducted using inductively coupled plasma (ICP, Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). This loading level is ≈10 times greater than that of previous Sn-SAMs materials based on the top-down approach.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0034_R_d45008328e1239" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Catal.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Remarkably, this innovative technology has also been effectively utilized to produce other atomic-level materials such as Sb, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, etc. (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), with extremely high metal loadings exceeding 10.0&nbsp;wt.% on the carbon matrix (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), far surpassing the majority of previous studies that used top-down methods preparing SAMs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0002">2g</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0009_R_d45008328e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun.">9</a>, <a href="#adma202412592-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0011_R_d45008328e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Environ. Sci.">11</a>-<a href="#adma202412592-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0013_R_d45008328e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">13</a>, <a href="#adma202412592-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0016_R_d45008328e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">16</a>, <a href="#adma202412592-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0017_R_d45008328e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">17</a>, <a href="#adma202412592-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0034_R_d45008328e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Catal.">34</a>-<a href="#adma202412592-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0041_R_d45008328e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The atomization process of SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles within the ring-like nested composite structure was visually observed by ex situ TEM (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The images reveal that, prior to pyrolysis, SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles are distinctly distributed within the middle layer of the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/SnO<sub>2</sub>/PDA composite structure. After heating to 700&nbsp;°C for 10&nbsp;min, the SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles within the nested composite diffuse into the surrounding matrix. Subsequent to a 20-min pyrolysis period, a noticeable reduction in both the number and size of SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles can be observed. Within 30&nbsp;min of thermal conversion, complete disintegration into very small nanoclusters is observed. Ultimately, after a total thermal degradation time of 40&nbsp;min, no traces of the initial SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles remain, confirming the successful atomization.</p>
            
            <p>To investigate the impact of metal oxide content in precursor nested structure on Sn atomization, samples with varying Sn densities were prepared by utilizing different doses of Sn precursors, denoted as Sn-SAMs@CNR-1, Sn-SAMs@CNR-2, and Sn-SAMs@CNR-3 correspondingly. Following pyrolysis at 700&nbsp;°C, the resulting Sn-SAMs@CNR-1 and Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 exhibit an amorphous structure, while weak diffraction peaks of SnO<sub>2</sub> are present in Sn-SAMs@CNR-3 in XRD patterns (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). High-resolution TEM (HRTEM, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information) analysis reveals the emergence of SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoclusters in the Sn-SAMs@CNR-3 with high loading of Sn, suggesting incomplete atomization due to excessive loading without sufficient N-containing substances for reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0042_R_d45008328e1295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, XRD patterns (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information) show that the absence of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> filling during high-temperature pyrolysis leads to the formation of a mixture of Sn, SnO, and SnO<sub>2</sub> in the product SnO<i><sub>x</sub></i>@CNR (<i>x</i> = 0,1,2), attributed to reduction by the outermost carbon layer on SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0043_R_d45008328e1318" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Chem. Eng. J.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This underscores the crucial role of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> in the atomization process. Based on TG analyses (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information) and conversion calculation results (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), the densities of Sn in samples Sn-SAMs@CNR-1, Sn-SAMs@CNR-2, and Sn-SAMs@CNR-3 are 8.0, 19.5, and 35.4&nbsp;wt.%, respectively. Subsequent validation through ICP testing (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) confirms the Sn loadings in these samples as 7.6, 18.9, and 32.8&nbsp;wt.%, respectively. The ICP results closely matched those of the TG test, and such a high loading of 18.9&nbsp;wt.% atomically dispersed Sn active sites, combined with the CNR<sub>S</sub> architecture, endows the composite with maximum active site exposure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0044_R_d45008328e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Energy Environ. Sci.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>Low-temperature nitrogen adsorption-desorption isotherms, along with pore size distribution curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrate that the CNR owns a specific surface area (SSA) of 658.7&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and contains numerous micropores and mesopores, providing ample sites for SAMs trapping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0045_R_d45008328e1353" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Appl. Catal. B Environ.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Conversely, the SSAs of Sn-SAMs@CNR-1, Sn-SAMs@CNR-2, and Sn-SAMs@CNR-3 are dropped to 437.9, 386.9, and 173.1&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The engagement of SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoclusters in the Sn-SAMs@CNR-3 sample significantly reduces its SSA compared to the other samples. These findings suggest efficient trapping and immobilization of Sn-SAMs by defect sites within the composites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0044_R_d45008328e1368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Energy Environ. Sci.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202412592-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202412592-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Performance of Sn-SAMs@CNR for Na<sup>+</sup> Storage</h3>
            
            <p>To evaluate the application potential of ultrahigh-loading Sn-SAMs, the electrochemical performance of Sn-SAMs@CNR as the anode for SIBs was systematically tested and compared with other excellent carbon-based and Sn-based materials. The cyclic voltammetry (CV, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information) curves for Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 show a wide peak at 0.9&nbsp;V during the initial cathodic cycle, which vanishes in later cycles due to the development of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) films.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0019_R_d45008328e1386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In subsequent cycles, the cathodic peak at 0.55&nbsp;V and the anodic peak at 0.32&nbsp;V correlate with the sodium ions insertion/extraction process within the carbon matrix.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0046_R_d45008328e1391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast for Sn-SAMs@CNR-3, the CV curve exhibits a peak of 1.25&nbsp;V at the anode, corresponding to the reduction of SnO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0047_R_d45008328e1399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">47</a>, <a href="#adma202412592-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0048_R_d45008328e1399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Energy Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The initial discharge/charge specific capacities (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information) of CNR, Sn-SAMs@CNR-1, Sn-SAMs@CNR-2, and Sn-SAMs@CNR-3 are 497/268, 521/330, 670/465, and 538/388&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> respectively, corresponding the ICE of 53.9%, 63.3%, 69.4%, and 72.1% respectively. The irreversible specific capacity is associated with the abundant defects present on the CNR substrate, and high defect densities are often correlated with low ICE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0049_R_d45008328e1412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Small Methods">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After 200 cycles at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 demonstrates the highest specific capacity of 430&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, surpassing 152&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> of CNR, as well as 251&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> of Sn-SAMs@CNR-1, and 355&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> of Sn-SAMs@CNR-3, respectively (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0003">3a</a>). The incorporation of high-loading Sn-SAMs into CNR leads to an increased number of active sites for sodium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0044_R_d45008328e1434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Energy Environ. Sci.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, despite the higher Sn loading, Sn-SAMs@CNR-3 fails to exhibit a correspondingly higher specific capacity due to severe agglomeration issues with SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoclusters.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0050_R_d45008328e1442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Energy Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The discharge/charge voltage curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0003">3b,c</a>) of Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 exhibit a noticeably higher and more stable specific capacity compared to CNR from the 2nd to the 100th cycle, indicating significantly enhanced capacity performance and robustness. The rate capability analyses in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0003">3d</a> reveal that the Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 electrode displays superior performance with specific capacities ranging from 462 to 248&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> as the current density increases from 0.1 to 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The electrode also maintains a capacity of 449&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density reverts to its initial state of 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The rate capability of Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 outperforms numerous recently reported carbon-based anodes in SIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0003">3e</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0020_R_d45008328e1468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#adma202412592-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0051_R_d45008328e1468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Small">51</a>-<a href="#adma202412592-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0056_R_d45008328e1468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Adv. Mater.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202412592-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8c7d5ca7-9d27-42ff-9d8f-a8e624cba294/adma202412592-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8c7d5ca7-9d27-42ff-9d8f-a8e624cba294/adma202412592-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/348c0313-c2dd-48e0-bb66-68e35b18381a/adma202412592-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8c7d5ca7-9d27-42ff-9d8f-a8e624cba294/adma202412592-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202412592-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202412592" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cycling performances of CNR, Sn-SAMs@CNR-1, Sn-SAMs@CNR-2, and Sn-SAMs@CNR-3 at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of b) Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 and c) CNR. d) Rate capability of various samples. e) Comparative analysis of rate capabilities of Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 and excellent carbon-based materials. f) Nyquist plots and g) long-cycling performances of different samples at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. h) Comparative analysis of cyclic stability between Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 and other representative carbon materials. i) Pseudocapacitance contribution of various samples at a scanning rate of 0.1–10.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. j) Cycling performances at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> of the full battery assembled with Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 versus NVP@C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Additionally, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0003">3f</a>) of Sn-SAMs@CNR-1 and Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 demonstrates the introduction of Sn-SAMs effectively mitigates charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and ion diffusion resistance compared to CNR. However, the impedance of Sn-SAMs@CNR-3 appears higher due to the relatively weaker conductivity associated with the semiconductor properties of SnO<sub>2</sub> clusters compared to that of Sn-SAMs. The extended cycle durability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0003">3g</a>) of Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> exhibits an impressive specific capacity of 364&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> over 5000 cycles, with a minimal capacity loss of only 0.00068% per cycle, which is surprisingly far superior to other carbon-based materials (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0003">3h</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information) and Sn-based materials (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0020_R_d45008328e1532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#adma202412592-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0052_R_d45008328e1532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Small">52</a>-<a href="#adma202412592-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0054_R_d45008328e1532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">54</a>, <a href="#adma202412592-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0057_R_d45008328e1532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Chem. Eng. J.">57</a>-<a href="#adma202412592-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0064_R_d45008328e1532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Adv. Energy Mater.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Comparatively, the remaining capacity of the other three materials is as follows: CNR (108&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), Sn-SAMs@CNR-1 (172&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and Sn-SAMs@CNR-3 (211&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). The enhanced electrochemical properties of Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 are credited to the incorporation of ultrahigh loading and all-region distribution of Sn-SAMs, coupled with a unique hollow porous ring-like structural matrix.</p>
            
            <p>To systematically analyze the sodium storage kinetics behavior of Sn-SAMs@CNR-2, CV curves were generated at various scanning rates (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). The consistent shape of the CV curve with increasing scanning rate suggests low polarization and excellent reliability of the Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0021_R_d45008328e1549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A <i>b</i> value nearing 1 suggests a surface pseudocapacitance-dominated procedure, whereas a <i>b</i> value approaching 0.5 signifies a diffusion-dominated mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0065_R_d45008328e1559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Adv. Energy Mater.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 electrode, the anode and cathode peaks (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information) present <i>b</i> values of 0.88 and 0.98, respectively, indicating the involvement of both pseudocapacitance and diffusion-controlled behaviors with pseudocapacitance being the dominant factor. The pseudocapacitance contribution (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0003">3i</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information) of the Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 electrode increases from 61.7% to 97.8% as the scanning rate is raised from 0.1 to 10&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, overtaking that of the other three samples and implying excellent kinetic behavior even under high rates. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information) illustrates the fitting curve for the capacitance contribution of the Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 electrode at a scanning rate of 2.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, highlighting a notably higher pseudocapacitance contribution compared to the CNR.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0065_R_d45008328e1583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Adv. Energy Mater.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To showcase the potential of Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 as a favorable anode material for SIBs, a coin-type full battery employing carbon-coated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP@C) as the cathode was fabricated. The NVP@C cathode shows a consistent capacity of ≈101&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). The capability of the Sn-SAMs@CNR-2//NVP@C full battery at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information), showcasing impressive initial charge and discharge capacities of 282 and 229&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, resulting in excellent ICE of 81.2%. The cycle test reveals that the capacity remains firm at 192&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> following 300 cycles conducted at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0003">3j</a>). Furthermore, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information) illustrates the functionality of the full battery at various rates, with the specific capacity remaining remarkably high at 108&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even at 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, showing exceptional rate performance.</p>
            
            <p>To comprehend the electrochemical reaction mechanism and structural robustness of the Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 electrode during the discharge/charge process, in situ Raman spectroscopy was performed, and two distinct broad peaks appeared in the first cycle of Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0004">4a</a>. Throughout the sodiation process, there is no significant change in the intensity of both peaks within the high-frequency region, with pseudocapacitance behavior dominating. However, a notable decrease in intensity of the D band and G band is observed after discharge reaches 2.0&nbsp;V, followed by stabilization in the low-frequency region after ≈1.0&nbsp;V, which is explained by the combined influence of adsorption-dominated pseudocapacitance and diffusion behavior.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0063_R_d45008328e1638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Adv. Energy Mater.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, a detailed analysis of Raman spectra at various charge-discharge states, presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0004">4b</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), reveals that the D1 peak reflects a disordered graphitic lattice while the <i>I</i><sub>D1</sub><i>/I</i><sub>G</sub> values indicate material defects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0066_R_d45008328e1655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - Adv. Mater.">66</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, the <i>I</i><sub>D1</sub><i>/I</i><sub>G</sub> values for Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 at the initial state, fully sodiated state, and fully desodiated state are 2.25, 1.72, and 2.14 respectively, suggesting a significant number of sodium adsorptions at defect sites during full sodiation and a return to the original state during desodiation. The symmetrical nature observed in Raman spectra further confirms excellent structural reversibility for Sn-SAMs@CNR-2. XPS tests conducted with various discharge and charge voltages reveal that the N 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0004">4c</a>) of the initial state exhibit four distinct peaks at 398.3, 399.3, 400.3, and 402.2&nbsp;eV, in line with pyridine-N, pyrrole-N, graphitic-N, and oxidized-N, respectively. The significant decrease in pyridine-N peak intensity during the discharge process, similar to CNR (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26a</a>, Supporting Information), can be attributed to the heightened reactivity of the N active species toward Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0021_R_d45008328e1675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the reversibility of the sodium storage peak in Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 remains significant even when charged to 3.0&nbsp;V, indicating that the incorporation of Sn-SAMs does not impact the adsorption capability of N active species for Na<sup>+</sup>. In the initial state for the C 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0004">4d</a>), distinct peaks are observed at frequencies of 284.6, 285.9, 287.2, 288.4, and 289.2&nbsp;eV, associated with sp<sup>3</sup>-C, sp<sup>2</sup>-C, C─N, CN/C─O, and CO, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0067_R_d45008328e1690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">67</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Following the discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V, the emergence of a new peak ≈289.8&nbsp;eV suggests the creation of an SEI layer along with various sodium-containing compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0021_R_d45008328e1695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Importantly, compared with CNR (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26b</a>, Supporting Information), the significant disappearance of the peak of 289.8&nbsp;eV in the corresponding spectrum of Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 when charging to 3.0&nbsp;V, manifests that the presence of Sn atoms effectively facilitates the reversibility of Na<sup>+</sup> storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0063_R_d45008328e1706" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Adv. Energy Mater.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The EIS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0004">4e,f</a>) was conducted to investigate the changes in impedance during the electrochemical reaction at different discharge and charge voltages. Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 consistently exhibit lower impedance values compared to CNR, especially when the discharge voltage is below 0.5&nbsp;V. This reduction in impedance can be linked to is related to the promotion of charge transfer by the introduction of Sn-SAMs with excellent conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0066_R_d45008328e1714" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - Adv. Mater.">66</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the Galvanostatic intermittent titration (GITT) was employed to analyze the diffusion dynamics engaged in sodiation/desodiation, as described in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0004">4g,h</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information). The Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 exhibits a notably higher diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>K</sub>) compared to CNR throughout the charge and discharge cycle.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0068_R_d45008328e1729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 68 - ACS Energy Lett.">68</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the EDS spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0004">4i–n</a>) illustrate that upon reaching a discharge voltage of 0.01&nbsp;V, elements such as Na in Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 are evenly distributed, suggesting that the incorporation of Sn-SAMs enhances the homogeneity of Na<sup>+</sup> storage within the carbon matrix.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0046_R_d45008328e1740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202412592-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1fd3a919-3f83-415d-a189-86396406c9b7/adma202412592-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1fd3a919-3f83-415d-a189-86396406c9b7/adma202412592-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3f4892e2-c6b4-47ee-b6e5-c9255bf0a877/adma202412592-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1fd3a919-3f83-415d-a189-86396406c9b7/adma202412592-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202412592-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202412592" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ Raman spectroscopy and b) Raman spectra of Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 at various discharge/charge stages during the initial cycle. c) N 1s and d) C 1s XPS spectra of Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 at various discharge/charge conditions during the initial cycle. Nyquist plots for e) CNR and f) Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 at varying discharge/charge conditions during the initial cycle. The GITT potential profiles of CNR and Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 in g) discharge and h) charge processes. i) HAADF-STEM image and EDS elemental mappings of j) carbon, k) nitrogen, l) oxygen, m) tin, and n) sodium for Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 after sodiation.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The above research findings suggest that Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 material offers multiple advantages for energy storage. The incorporation of high-loading Sn-SAMs in Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 materials elevates sodium storage capacity by utilizing active sites and improving reaction kinetics. Moreover, the robust Sn-N<sub>4</sub> coordination structure enhances electron transfer efficiency, improves the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup>, and prevents Sn-SAMs accumulation during cycling, leading to exceptional long-term performance. The intricately designed porous nanostructure with slender walls and hollow morphology of the CNRs matrix promotes efficient Na<sup>+</sup> ion contact with the electrolyte and mitigates volumetric expansion. Consequently, the Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 electrode demonstrates superior and unprecedented electrochemical sodium storage performance.</p>
            
            <p>The advantages of sodium storage of the Sn-SAMs@CNR-2 electrode were further elucidated through DFT calculations. The study involves constructing three computational models, including perfect graphene (PG), low loading Sn-SAMs graphene (LSnG), and high loading Sn-SAMs graphene (HSnG), as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a> (Supporting Information). The adsorption energy (<i>E</i><sub>ad</sub>) for a Na atom on PG, LSnG, and HSnG is calculated to be −1.25, −1.85, and −2.62&nbsp;eV, respectively (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0005">5a–c</a>). Computational results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0005">5g</a>) demonstrate that compared with PG, the introduction of Sn–N<sub>4</sub> structure facilitates more adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> in the model, and further the model with high-density Sn–N<sub>4</sub> exhibits better adsorption than the model with low Sn–N<sub>4</sub> content. The charge density analyses (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0005">5d–f</a>) reveal that graphene with Sn-SAMs can effectively adsorb Na via the transfer of charge from the adsorbed Na to the adjacent N atoms, especially the HSnG exhibits a more obviously enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption capacity. Bader charge analyses indicate that incorporating Sn-SAMs can result in a more substantial charge loss for Na, with higher doping levels of Sn-SAMs resulting in more significant charge loss for Na, and additional electrons acquired by N at the substrate, as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information). These findings confirm that HSnG can effectively improve the interaction of Na<sup>+</sup> with the active substrate. Further analysis of local Fermi softness (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0005">5h</a>) was conducted to assess the reactivity of active sites in three models, with the value representing spatial reactivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202412592-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202412592-bib-0069_R_d45008328e1814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 69 - Energy Environ. Sci.">69</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The incorporation of Sn-SAMs promotes the creation of an increased quantity of active sites. Moreover, the introduction of multiple Sn-SAMs leads to a notable enhancement of N activity within the substrate, aligning with the adsorption energy and Bader analysis outcomes. Additionally, the density of states (DOS, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202412592-fig-0005">5i</a>) near the Fermi level for HSnG surpasses that of PG and LSnG, indicating high single-atom doping enhances electron conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption capacity. These DFT results provide robust evidence that graphene with ultrahigh Sn-SAMs loading offers distinct advantages in sodium storage by regulating the electronic structure and enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption capacity.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202412592-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/498c1eeb-87ef-443a-ad64-b14f1b9801d8/adma202412592-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/498c1eeb-87ef-443a-ad64-b14f1b9801d8/adma202412592-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/13427a35-a048-47c5-b0ad-b61c1cb90530/adma202412592-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/498c1eeb-87ef-443a-ad64-b14f1b9801d8/adma202412592-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202412592-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202412592" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Top and side perspectives of the simulation of Na atom adsorption on a) perfect graphene, b) low loading Sn-SAMs graphene, and c) high loading Sn-SAMs graphene. Brown, blue, grey, and yellow spheres represent C, N, Sn, and Na atoms, respectively. d–f) The associated electron density differences with an isosurface level of 0.002 electron borh<sup>−3</sup>. In this representation, yellow signifies increases in electron density, while blue denotes decreases. g) The adsorption energy (<i>E</i><sub>ad</sub>, eV) of a single Na atom adsorption on three frames. h) The heat map depicting sectional reactivity for the active sites across the three frames, where red signifies the higher, while blue denotes lower reactivity. i) The DOS of three computational models.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202412592-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202412592-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the universal and scalable oriented diffusion strategy for the preparation of carbon-supported various single atoms with ultrahigh loading has been presented. Under spatial confinement using g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanorings as templates and layer-by-layer tactics, all-region ultrahigh loading of Sn-SAMs of 18.9&nbsp;wt.% over CNRs matrix can be achieved. Notably, this strategy is also applied to other metal materials (Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, and Sb) with high loading over 10.0&nbsp;wt.%. Detailed characterization and theoretical simulations demonstrate the high-density Sn-N<sub>4</sub> incorporation effectively regulates the electron structure and reaction kinetics of CNRs for Na<sup>+</sup> storage. Consequently, the Sn-SAMs@CNR electrode shows outstanding durability of 364&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even after 5000 cycles coupled with an impressively 0.00068% capacity decay per cycle, outperforming most of the previously reported carbonaceous and Sn-based anodes. This innovative pathway favors the development of high-loading single-atom materials in different fields, and the comprehensive analyses of high-density SAMs-doping CNRs would contribute to understanding high-performance energy storage systems.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
